alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="665" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/FoggDam-NT.jpg/220px-FoggDam-NT.jpg" field</div></div></div>\n<p>A <b>thunderstorm</b>, also known as an <b>electrical storm</b> or a <b>lightning storm</b>, is a <a href="/wiki/Storm" title="Storm">storm</a> characterized by the presence of <a href="/wiki/Lightning" title="Lightning">lightning</a> and its <a href="/wiki/Acoustics" title="Acoustics">acoustic</a> effect on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere" title="Earth\'s atmosphere">Earth\'s atmosphere</a>, known as <a href="/wiki/Thunder" title="Thunder">thunder</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> Relatively weak thunderstorms are sometimes called <b>thundershowers</b>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> Thunderstorms occur in a type of <a href="/wiki/Cloud" title="Cloud">cloud</a> known as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cumulonimbus" title="Cumulonimbus">cumulonimbus</a>. They are usually accompanied by <a href="/wiki/Wind" title="Wind">strong winds</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heavy_rain_(meteorology)" title="Heavy rain (meteorology)">heavy rain</a>, and sometimes <a href="/wiki/Thundersnow" title="Thundersnow">snow</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ice_pellets" title="Ice pellets">sleet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hail" title="Hail">hail</a>, or, in contrast, <a href="/wiki/Dry_thunderstorm" title="Dry thunderstorm">no precipitation</a> at all. Thunderstorms may <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thunderstorm_training" title="Thunderstorm training">line up in a series</a> or become a <a href="/wiki/Rainband" title="Rainband">rainband</a>, known as a <a href="/wiki/Squall_line" title="Squall line">squall line</a>. Strong or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Severe_thunderstorm" title="Severe thunderstorm">severe thunderstorms</a> include some of the most dangerous weather phenomena, including large hail, strong winds, and <a href="/wiki/Tornado" title="Tornado">tornadoes</a>. Some of the most persistent severe thunderstorms, known as <a href="/wiki/Supercell" title="Supercell">supercells</a>, rotate as do cyclones. While most thunderstorms move with the mean wind flow through the layer of the <a href="/wiki/Troposphere" title="Troposphere">troposphere</a> that they occupy, vertical <a href="/wiki/Wind_shear" title="Wind shear">wind shear</a> sometimes causes a deviation in their course at a right angle to the wind shear direction.\n</p><p>Thunderstorms result from the rapid upward movement of warm, moist air, sometimes along a <a href="/wiki/Weather_front" title="Weather front">front</a>. As the warm, moist air moves upward, it cools, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Condense" title="Condense">condenses</a>, and forms a cumulonimbus cloud that can reach heights of over 20 kilometres (12&#160;mi). As the rising air reaches its <a href="/wiki/Dew_point" title="Dew point">dew point</a> temperature, water vapor condenses into water droplets or ice, reducing pressure locally within the thunderstorm cell. Any precipitation falls the long distance through the clouds towards the Earth\'s surface. As the droplets fall, they collide with other droplets and become larger. The falling droplets create a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Downdraft" title="Downdraft">downdraft</a> as it pulls cold air with it, and this cold air spreads out at the Earth\'s surface, occasionally causing strong winds that are commonly associated with thunderstorms.\n</p><p>Thunderstorms can form and develop in any geographic location but most frequently within the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mid-latitudes" title="Mid-latitudes">mid-latitude</a>, where warm, moist air from tropical latitudes collides with cooler air from polar latitudes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nssl.noaa.gov_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-nssl.noaa.gov-3">[3]</a></sup> Thunderstorms are responsible for the development and formation of many severe weather phenomena. Thunderstorms, and the phenomena that occur along with them, pose great hazards. Damage that results from thunderstorms is mainly inflicted by <a href="/wiki/Downburst" title="Downburst">downburst</a> winds, large hailstones, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flash_flooding" title="Flash flooding">flash flooding</a> caused by heavy <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Precipitation_(meteorology)" title="Precipitation (meteorology)">precipitation</a>. Stronger thunderstorm cells are capable of producing tornadoes and <a href="/wiki/Waterspout" title="Waterspout">waterspouts</a>.\n</p><p>There are four types of thunderstorms: <a href="/wiki/Air-mass_thunderstorm" title="Air-mass thunderstorm">single-cell</a>, <a href="/wiki/Multicellular_thunderstorm" title="Multicellular thunderstorm">multi-cell cluster</a>, multi-cell lines and supercells. Supercell thunderstorms are the strongest and most severe. <a href="/wiki/Mesoscale_convective_system" title="Mesoscale convective system">Mesoscale convective systems</a> formed by favorable vertical wind shear within the <a href="/wiki/Tropics" title="Tropics">tropics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Subtropics" title="Subtropics">subtropics</a> can be responsible for the development of <a href="/wiki/Tropical_cyclone" title="Tropical cyclone">hurricanes</a>. <a href="/wiki/Dry_thunderstorm" title="Dry thunderstorm">Dry thunderstorms</a>, with no precipitation, can cause the outbreak of <a href="/wiki/Wildfire" title="Wildfire">wildfires</a> from the heat generated from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cloud-to-ground_lightning" title="Cloud-to-ground lightning">cloud-to-ground lightning</a> that accompanies them. Several means are used to study thunderstorms: <a href="/wiki/Weather_radar" title="Weather radar">weather radar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Weather_station" title="Weather station">weather stations</a>, and video photography. Past civilizations held various myths concerning thunderstorms and their development as late as the <a href="/wiki/18th_century" title="18th century">18th century</a>. Beyond the Earth\'s atmosphere, thunderstorms have also been observed on the planets of <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a>, and, probably, <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" class="tocnumber">1.3</span> <span class="toctext">Dissipating stage</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-5"><a href="#Classification"><span class="tocnumber">2</span> <span class="toctext">Classification</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-6"><a href="#Single-cell"><span class="tocnumber">2.1</span> <span class="toctext">Single-cell</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-7"><a href="#Multi-cell_clusters"><span class="tocnumber">2.2</span> <span class="toctext">Multi-cell clusters</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-8"><a href="#Multicell_lines"><span class="tocnumber">2.3</span> <span class="toctext">Multicell lines</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-9"><a href="#Supercells"><span class="tocnumber">2.4</span> <span class="toctext">Supercells</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-10"><a href="#Severe_thunderstorms"><span class="tocnumber">2.5</span> <span class="toctext">Severe thunderstorms</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-11"><a href="#Mesoscale_convective_systems"><span class="tocnumber">2.6</span> <span class="toctext">Mesoscale convective systems</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-12"><a href="#Motion"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Motion</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-13"><a href="#Back-building_thunderstorm"><span class="tocnumber">3.1</span> <span class="toctext">Back-building thunderstorm</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-14"><a href="#Hazards"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Hazards</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-15"><a href="#Cloud-to-ground_lightning"><span class="tocnumber">4.1</span> <span class="toctext">Cloud-to-ground lightning</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-16"><a href="#Hail"><span class="tocnumber">4.2</span> <span class="toctext">Hail</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-17"><a href="#Tornadoes_and_waterspouts"><span class="tocnumber">4.3</span> <span class="toctext">Tornadoes and waterspouts</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-18"><a href="#Flash_flood"><span class="tocnumber">4.4</span> <span class="toctext">Flash flood</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-19"><a href="#Downburst"><span class="tocnumber">4.5</span> <span class="toctext">Downburst</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-20"><a href="#Thunderstorm_asthma"><span class="tocnumber">4.6</span> <span class="toctext">Thunderstorm asthma</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-21"><a href="#Safety_precautions"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Safety precautions</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-22"><a href="#Preparedness"><span class="tocnumber">5.1</span> <span class="toctext">Preparedness</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-23"><a href="#Safety"><span class="tocnumber">5.2</span> <span class="toctext">Safety</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-24"><a href="#Frequent_occurrences"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Frequent occurrences</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-25"><a href="#Energy"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Energy</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-26"><a href="#Studies"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Studies</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-27"><a href="#Mythology_and_religion"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Mythology and religion</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-28"><a href="#Outside_of_Earth"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Outside of Earth</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-29"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-30"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-31"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-32"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Life_cycle">Life cycle</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thunderstorm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Life cycle">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:442px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Thunderstorm_formation.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="554" data-file-width="1087" decoding="async" height="224" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Thunderstorm_formation.jpg/440px-Thunderstorm_formation.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Thunderstorm_formation.jpg/660px-Thunderstorm_formation.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Thunderstorm_formation.jpg/880px-Thunderstorm_formation.jpg 2x" width="440"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Thunderstorm_formation.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Stages of a thunderstorm\'s life.</div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Cloud" title="Cloud">Cloud</a></div>\n<p>Warm air has a lower <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">density</a> than cool air, so warmer air rises upwards and cooler air will settle at the bottom<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> (this effect can be seen with a <a href="/wiki/Hot_air_balloon" title="Hot air balloon">hot air balloon</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> Clouds form as relatively warmer air, carrying moisture, rises within cooler air. The moist air rises, and, as it does so, it cools and some of the <a href="/wiki/Water_vapor" title="Water vapor">water vapor</a> in that rising air <a href="/wiki/Condensation" title="Condensation">condenses</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> When the moisture condenses, it releases energy known as <a href="/wiki/Latent_heat" title="Latent heat">latent heat</a> of condensation, which allows the rising packet of air to cool less than the cooler surrounding air<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> continuing the cloud\'s ascension. If enough <a href="/wiki/Convective_available_potential_energy" title="Convective available potential energy">instability</a> is present in the atmosphere, this process will continue long enough for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cumulonimbus" title="Cumulonimbus">cumulonimbus</a> clouds to form and produce <a href="/wiki/Lightning" title="Lightning">lightning</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thunder" title="Thunder">thunder</a>. Meteorological indices such as <a href="/wiki/Convective_available_potential_energy" title="Convective available potential energy">convective available potential energy</a> (CAPE) and the <a href="/wiki/Lifted_index" title="Lifted index">lifted index</a> can be used to assist in determining potential upward vertical development of clouds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CAPE_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-CAPE-8">[8]</a></sup> Generally, thunderstorms require three conditions to form:\n</p>\n<ol><li>Moisture</li>\n<li>An unstable airmass</li>\n<li>A lifting force (heat)</li></ol>\n<p>All thunderstorms, regardless of type, go through three stages: the <b>developing stage</b>, the <b>mature stage</b>, and the <b>dissipation stage</b>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Extreme_Weather_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Extreme_Weather-9">[9]</a></sup> The average thunderstorm has a 24&#160;km (15&#160;mi) diameter. Depending on the conditions present in the atmosphere, each of these three stages take an average of 30 minutes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tsbasics_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-tsbasics-10">[10]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Developing_stage">Developing stage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thunderstorm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Developing stage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The first stage of a thunderstorm is the cumulus stage or developing stage. During this stage, masses of moisture are lifted upwards into the atmosphere. The trigger for this lift can be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Insolation" title="Insolation">solar illumination</a>, where the heating of the ground produces <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thermals" title="Thermals">thermals</a>, or where two winds converge forcing air upwards, or where winds blow over terrain of increasing elevation. The moisture carried upward cools into liquid drops of water due to lower temperatures at high altitude, which appear as <i><a href="/wiki/Cumulus_cloud" title="Cumulus cloud">cumulus</a></i> clouds. As the water vapor condenses into liquid, <a href="/wiki/Latent_heat" title="Latent heat">latent heat</a> is released, which warms the air, causing it to become less dense than the surrounding, drier air. The air tends to rise in an <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Updraft" title="Updraft">updraft</a></i> through the process of <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_convection" title="Atmospheric convection">convection</a> (hence the term <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Precipitation_(meteorology)" title="Precipitation (meteorology)">convective precipitation</a>). This process creates a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Low_pressure_area" title="Low pressure area">low-pressure zone</a> within and beneath the forming thunderstorm. In a typical thunderstorm, approximately 500 million kilograms of water vapor are lifted into the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere" title="Earth\'s atmosphere">Earth\'s atmosphere</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-condensationenergy_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-condensationenergy-11">[11]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mature_stage">Mature stage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thunderstorm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Mature stage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Anvil_shaped_cumulus_panorama_edit_crop.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="965" data-file-width="2348" decoding="async" height="90" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Anvil_shaped_cumulus_panorama_edit_crop.jpg/220px-Anvil_shaped_cumulus_panorama_edit_crop.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Anvil_shaped_cumulus_panorama_edit_crop.jpg/330px-Anvil_shaped_cumulus_panorama_edit_crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Anvil_shaped_cumulus_panorama_edit_crop.jpg/440px-Anvil_shaped_cumulus_panorama_edit_crop.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Anvil_shaped_cumulus_panorama_edit_crop.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Anvil-shaped thundercloud in the mature stage</div></div></div>\n<p>In the mature stage of a thunderstorm, the warmed air continues to rise until it reaches an area of warmer air and can rise no farther. Often this \'cap\' is the <a href="/wiki/Tropopause" title="Tropopause">tropopause</a>. The air is instead forced to spread out, giving the storm a characteristic <a href="/wiki/Anvil" title="Anvil">anvil</a> shape. The resulting cloud is called <i><a href="/wiki/Cumulonimbus_incus" title="Cumulonimbus incus">cumulonimbus incus</a></i>. The water droplets <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coalescence_(meteorology)" title="Coalescence (meteorology)">coalesce</a> into larger and heavier droplets and freeze to become ice particles. As these fall, they melt to become <a href="/wiki/Rain" title="Rain">rain</a>. If the updraft is strong enough, the droplets are held aloft long enough to become so large that they do not melt completely but fall as <a href="/wiki/Hail" title="Hail">hail</a>. While updrafts are still present, the falling rain drags the surrounding air with it, creating <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Downdrafts" title="Downdrafts">downdrafts</a></i> as well. The simultaneous presence of both an updraft and a downdraft marks the mature stage of the storm and produces cumulonimbus clouds. During this stage, considerable internal <a href="/wiki/Turbulence" title="Turbulence">turbulence</a> can occur within, which manifests as strong winds, severe lightning, and even <a href="/wiki/Tornado" title="Tornado">tornadoes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Typically, if there is little <a href="/wiki/Wind_shear" title="Wind shear">wind shear</a>, the storm will rapidly enter the dissipating stage and \'rain itself out\',<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Extreme_Weather_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-Extreme_Weather-9">[9]</a></sup> but, if there is sufficient change in wind speed or direction, the downdraft will be separated from the updraft, and the storm may become a <a href="/wiki/Supercell" title="Supercell">supercell</a>, where the mature stage can sustain itself for several hours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-motion_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-motion-13">[13]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dissipating_stage">Dissipating stage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thunderstorm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Dissipating stage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Single-cell_Thunderstorm_in_a_No-shear_Environment..jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2432" data-file-width="4320" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Single-cell_Thunderstorm_in_a_No-shear_Environment..jpg/220px-Single-cell_Thunderstorm_in_a_No-shear_Environment..jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Single-cell_Thunderstorm_in_a_No-shear_Environment..jpg/330px-Single-cell_Thunderstorm_in_a_No-shear_Environment..jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Single-cell_Thunderstorm_in_a_No-shear_Environment..jpg/440px-Single-cell_Thunderstorm_in_a_No-shear_Environment..jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Single-cell_Thunderstorm_in_a_No-shear_Environment..jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A thunderstorm in an environment with no winds to shear the storm or blow the anvil in any one direction</div></div></div>\n<p>In the dissipation stage, the thunderstorm is dominated by the downdraft. If atmospheric conditions do not support super cellular development, this stage occurs rather quickly, approximately 20&#8211;30 minutes into the life of the thunderstorm. The downdraft will push down out of the thunderstorm, hit the ground and spread out. This phenomenon is known as a <a href="/wiki/Downburst" title="Downburst">downburst</a>. The cool air carried to the ground by the downdraft cuts off the inflow of the thunderstorm, the updraft disappears and the thunderstorm will dissipate. Thunderstorms in an atmosphere with virtually no vertical wind shear weaken as soon as they send out an outflow boundary in all directions, which then quickly cuts off its <a href="/wiki/Inflow_(meteorology)" title="Inflow (meteorology)">inflow</a> of relatively warm, moist air, and kills the thunderstorm\'s further growth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> The downdraft hitting the ground creates an <a href="/wiki/Outflow_boundary" title="Outflow boundary">outflow boundary</a>. This can cause downbursts, a potential hazardous condition for aircraft to fly through, as a substantial change in wind speed and direction occurs, resulting in a decrease of airspeed and the subsequent reduction in lift for the aircraft. The stronger the <a href="/wiki/Outflow_boundary" title="Outflow boundary">outflow boundary</a> is, the stronger the resultant vertical wind shear becomes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thunderstorm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Classification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CAPE_vs_SHEAR.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="456" data-file-width="777" decoding="async" height="129" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/CAPE_vs_SHEAR.png/220px-CAPE_vs_SHEAR.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/CAPE_vs_SHEAR.png/330px-CAPE_vs_SHEAR.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/CAPE_vs_SHEAR.png/440px-CAPE_vs_SHEAR.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CAPE_vs_SHEAR.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Conditions favorable for thunderstorm types and complexes</div></div></div>\n<p>There are four main types of thunderstorms: single-cell, multi-cell, squall line (also called multi-cell line) and supercell. Which type forms depends on the instability and relative wind conditions at different layers of the atmosphere ("<a href="/wiki/Wind_shear" title="Wind shear">wind shear</a>"). Single-cell thunderstorms form in environments of low vertical wind shear and last only 20&#8211;30 minutes.\n</p><p>Organized thunderstorms and thunderstorm clusters/lines can have longer life cycles as they form in environments of significant vertical wind shear, normally greater than 25 knots (13&#160;m/s) in the lowest 6 kilometres (3.7&#160;mi) of the <a href="/wiki/Troposphere" title="Troposphere">troposphere</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> which aids the development of stronger updrafts as well as various forms of severe weather. The supercell is the strongest of the thunderstorms, most commonly associated with large hail, high winds, and tornado formation.  <a href="/wiki/Precipitable_water" title="Precipitable water">Precipitable water</a> values of greater than 31.8 millimetres (1.25&#160;in) favor the development of organized thunderstorm complexes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>  Those with heavy rainfall normally have precipitable water values greater than 36.9 millimetres (1.45&#160;in).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>  Upstream values of <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_instability#CAPE_and_CIN" title="Atmospheric instability">CAPE</a> of greater than 800 J/kg are usually required for the development of organized convection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Single-cell">Single-cell</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thunderstorm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Single-cell">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Air-mass_thunderstorm" title="Air-mass thunderstorm">Air-mass thunderstorm</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Thunderstorm_over_Wagga_Wagga.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="846" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Thunderstorm_over_Wagga_Wagga.jpg/170px-Thunderstorm_over_Wagga_Wagga.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Thunderstorm_over_Wagga_Wagga.jpg/255px-Thunderstorm_over_Wagga_Wagga.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Thunderstorm_over_Wagga_Wagga.jpg/340px-Thunderstorm_over_Wagga_Wagga.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Thunderstorm_over_Wagga_Wagga.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A single-cell thunderstorm over <a href="/wiki/Wagga_Wagga" title="Wagga Wagga">Wagga Wagga</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>This term technically applies to a single thunderstorm with one main updraft. Also known as <a href="/wiki/Air-mass_thunderstorm" title="Air-mass thunderstorm">air-mass thunderstorms</a>, these are the typical summer thunderstorms in many temperate locales. They also occur in the cool unstable air that often follows the passage of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weather_fronts" title="Weather fronts">cold front</a> from the sea during winter. Within a cluster of thunderstorms, the term "cell" refers to each separate principal updraft. Thunderstorm cells occasionally form in isolation, as the occurrence of one thunderstorm can develop an outflow boundary that sets up new thunderstorm development. Such storms are rarely severe and are a result of local atmospheric instability; hence the term "air mass thunderstorm". When such storms have a brief period of severe weather associated with them, it is known as a pulse severe storm. Pulse severe storms are poorly organized and occur randomly in time and space, making them difficult to forecast. Single-cell thunderstorms normally last 20&#8211;30&#160;minutes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tsbasics_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-tsbasics-10">[10]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Multi-cell_clusters">Multi-cell clusters</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thunderstorm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Multi-cell clusters">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Multicellular_thunderstorm" title="Multicellular thunderstorm">Multicellular thunderstorm</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:8402_STS41B_Challenger_Thunderstorms_over_Brazil.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/8402_STS41B_Challenger_Thunderstorms_over_Brazil.JPG/220px-8402_STS41B_Challenger_Thunderstorms_over_Brazil.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/8402_STS41B_Challenger_Thunderstorms_over_Brazil.JPG/330px-8402_STS41B_Challenger_Thunderstorms_over_Brazil.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/8402_STS41B_Challenger_Thunderstorms_over_Brazil.JPG/440px-8402_STS41B_Challenger_Thunderstorms_over_Brazil.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:8402_STS41B_Challenger_Thunderstorms_over_Brazil.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A group of thunderstorms over <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a> photographed by the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Challenger" title="Space Shuttle Challenger">Space Shuttle Challenger</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>This is the most common type of thunderstorm development. <i>Mature thunderstorms</i> are found near the center of the cluster, while dissipating thunderstorms exist on their downwind side. <i>Multicell storms</i> form as clusters of storms but may then evolve into one or more <a href="/wiki/Squall_line" title="Squall line">squall lines</a>. While each cell of the cluster may only last 20&#160;minutes, the cluster itself may persist for hours at a time. They often arise from convective updrafts in or near mountain ranges and linear weather boundaries, such as strong cold fronts or troughs of low pressure. These type of storms are stronger than the single-cell storm, yet much weaker than the supercell storm. Hazards with the multicell cluster include moderate-sized hail, flash flooding, and weak tornadoes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tsbasics_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-tsbasics-10">[10]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Multicell_lines">Multicell lines</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thunderstorm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Multicell lines">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Squall_line" title="Squall line">Squall line</a></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_derecho_events" title="List of derecho events">List of derecho events</a></div>\n<p>A squall line is an elongated line of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thunderstorms#Severe_thunderstorms" title="Thunderstorms">severe thunderstorms</a> that can form along or ahead of a <a href="/wiki/Cold_front" title="Cold front">cold front</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> In the early 20th century, the term was used as a synonym for <a href="/wiki/Cold_front" title="Cold front">cold front</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OU_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-OU-22">[22]</a></sup> The squall line contains heavy <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Precipitation_(meteorology)" title="Precipitation (meteorology)">precipitation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hail" title="Hail">hail</a>, frequent <a href="/wiki/Lightning" title="Lightning">lightning</a>, strong straight line <a href="/wiki/Wind" title="Wind">winds</a>, and possibly <a href="/wiki/Tornado" title="Tornado">tornadoes</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Waterspouts" title="Waterspouts">waterspouts</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Severe_weather" title="Severe weather">Severe weather</a> in the form of strong straight-line winds can be expected in areas where the squall line itself is in the shape of a <a href="/wiki/Bow_echo" title="Bow echo">bow echo</a>, within the portion of the line that bows out the most.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Tornado" title="Tornado">Tornadoes</a> can be found along waves within a <a href="/wiki/Line_echo_wave_pattern" title="Line echo wave pattern">line echo wave pattern</a>, or LEWP, where mesoscale <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Low_pressure_area" title="Low pressure area">low pressure areas</a> are present.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Some bow echoes in the summer are called <a href="/wiki/Derecho" title="Derecho">derechos</a>, and move quite fast through large sections of territory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-noaa_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-noaa-26">[26]</a></sup> On the back edge of the rain shield associated with mature squall lines, a <a href="/wiki/Wake_low" title="Wake low">wake low</a> can form, which is a mesoscale low pressure area that forms behind the mesoscale high pressure system normally present under the rain canopy, which are sometimes associated with a <a href="/wiki/Heat_burst" title="Heat burst">heat burst</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> This kind of storm is also known as "Wind of the Stony Lake" (Traditional Chinese:&#30707;&#28246;&#39080; &#8211; shi2 hu2 feng1, Simplified Chinese: &#30707;&#28246;&#39118;) in southern China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Supercells">Supercells</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thunderstorm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Supercells">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Supercell" title="Supercell">Supercell</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chaparral_Supercell_2.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1920" data-file-width="2560" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Chaparral_Supercell_2.JPG/220px-Chaparral_Supercell_2.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Chaparral_Supercell_2.JPG/330px-Chaparral_Supercell_2.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Chaparral_Supercell_2.JPG/440px-Chaparral_Supercell_2.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chaparral_Supercell_2.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Supercell" title="Supercell">supercell</a> thunderstorm over <a href="/wiki/Chaparral" title="Chaparral">Chaparral</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico" title="New Mexico">New Mexico</a>.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Thunderhead.anvil.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1528" data-file-width="2421" decoding="async" height="139" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Thunderhead.anvil.jpg/220px-Thunderhead.anvil.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Thunderhead.anvil.jpg/330px-Thunderhead.anvil.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Thunderhead.anvil.jpg/440px-Thunderhead.anvil.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Thunderhead.anvil.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The setting sun illuminates the top of a classic anvil-shaped thunderstorm cloud in eastern <a href="/wiki/Nebraska" title="Nebraska">Nebraska</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Supercell storms are large, usually <a href="/wiki/Severe_weather" title="Severe weather">severe</a>, quasi-steady-state storms that form in an environment where wind speed or wind direction varies with height ("<a href="/wiki/Wind_shear" title="Wind shear">wind shear</a>"), and they have separate downdrafts and updrafts (i.e., where its associated precipitation is not falling through the updraft) with a strong, rotating updraft (a "<a href="/wiki/Mesocyclone" title="Mesocyclone">mesocyclone</a>"). These storms normally have such powerful updrafts that the top of the supercell storm cloud (or anvil) can break through the <a href="/wiki/Troposphere" title="Troposphere">troposphere</a> and reach into the lower levels of the <a href="/wiki/Stratosphere" title="Stratosphere">stratosphere</a>, and supercell storms can be 24 kilometres (15&#160;mi) wide. Research has shown that at least 90&#160;percent of supercells cause <a href="/wiki/Severe_weather" title="Severe weather">severe weather</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-motion_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-motion-13">[13]</a></sup> These storms can produce destructive <a href="/wiki/Tornado" title="Tornado">tornadoes</a>, extremely large <a href="/wiki/Hail" title="Hail">hailstones</a> (10 centimetres or 4 inches diameter), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Straight-line_wind" title="Straight-line wind">straight-line winds</a> in excess of 130&#160;km/h (81&#160;mph), and <a href="/wiki/Flash_flood" title="Flash flood">flash floods</a>. In fact, research has shown that most tornadoes occur from this type of thunderstorm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> Supercells are generally the strongest type of thunderstorm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tsbasics_10-3"><a href="#cite_note-tsbasics-10">[10]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Severe_thunderstorms">Severe thunderstorms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thunderstorm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Severe thunderstorms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In the United States, a thunderstorm is classed as severe if winds reach at least 93 kilometres per hour (58&#160;mph), hail is 25 millimetres (1&#160;in) in diameter or larger, or if <a href="/wiki/Funnel_cloud" title="Funnel cloud">funnel clouds</a> or <a href="/wiki/Tornado" title="Tornado">tornadoes</a> are reported.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> Although a funnel cloud or tornado indicates a severe thunderstorm, a <a href="/wiki/Tornado_warning" title="Tornado warning">tornado warning</a> is issued in place of a <a href="/wiki/Severe_thunderstorm_warning" title="Severe thunderstorm warning">severe thunderstorm warning</a>. A severe thunderstorm warning is issued if a thunderstorm becomes severe, or will soon turn severe. In Canada, a rainfall rate greater than 50 millimetres (2&#160;in) in one hour, or 75 millimetres (3&#160;in) in three hours, is also used to indicate severe thunderstorms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>  Severe thunderstorms can occur from any type of storm cell. However, <a href="/wiki/Multicellular_thunderstorm" title="Multicellular thunderstorm">multicell</a>, <a href="/wiki/Supercell" title="Supercell">supercell</a>, and squall lines represent the most common forms of thunderstorms that produce severe weather.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-motion_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-motion-13">[13]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mesoscale_convective_systems">Mesoscale convective systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thunderstorm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Mesoscale convective systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Mesoscale_convective_system" title="Mesoscale convective system">Mesoscale convective system</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:132px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MCCaug0220060545z.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="73" data-file-width="130" decoding="async" height="73" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/94/MCCaug0220060545z.gif" width="130"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MCCaug0220060545z.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>MCC moving through <a href="/wiki/New_England" title="New England">New England</a>: August 2, 2006 0600 UTC</div></div></div>\n<p>A <a href="/wiki/Mesoscale_convective_system" title="Mesoscale convective system">mesoscale convective system</a> (MCS) is a complex of thunderstorms that becomes organized on a scale larger than the individual thunderstorms but smaller than <a href="/wiki/Extratropical_cyclone" title="Extratropical cyclone">extratropical cyclones</a>, and normally persists for several hours or more.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>  A mesoscale convective system\'s overall cloud and precipitation pattern may be round or linear in shape, and include weather systems such as <a href="/wiki/Tropical_cyclone" title="Tropical cyclone">tropical cyclones</a>, <a href="/wiki/Squall_line" title="Squall line">squall lines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lake-effect_snow" title="Lake-effect snow">lake-effect snow</a> events, <a href="/wiki/Polar_low" title="Polar low">polar lows</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mesoscale_Convective_Complex" title="Mesoscale Convective Complex">Mesoscale Convective Complexes</a> (MCCs), and they generally form near <a href="/wiki/Weather_front" title="Weather front">weather fronts</a>. Most mesoscale convective systems develop overnight and continue their lifespan through the next day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Extreme_Weather_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-Extreme_Weather-9">[9]</a></sup> The type that forms during the warm season over land has been noted across <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a>, <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a>, with a maximum in activity noted during the late afternoon and evening hours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Forms of MCS that develop in the tropics are found in use either the <a href="/wiki/Intertropical_Convergence_Zone" title="Intertropical Convergence Zone">Intertropical Convergence Zone</a> or <a href="/wiki/Monsoon_trough" title="Monsoon trough">monsoon troughs</a>, generally within the warm season between <a href="/wiki/Spring_(season)" title="Spring (season)">spring</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fall" title="Fall">fall</a>. More intense systems form over land than over water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> One exception is that of <a href="/wiki/Lake-effect_snow" title="Lake-effect snow">lake-effect snow</a> bands, which form due to cold air moving across relatively warm bodies of water, and occurs from fall through spring.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>  Polar lows are a second special class of MCS. They form at high latitudes during the cold season.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> Once the parent MCS dies, later thunderstorm development can occur in connection with its remnant <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mesoscale_convective_vortex" title="Mesoscale convective vortex">mesoscale convective vortex</a> (MCV).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>  Mesoscale convective systems are important to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_rainfall_climatology" title="United States rainfall climatology">United States rainfall climatology</a> over the <a href="/wiki/Great_Plains" title="Great Plains">Great Plains</a> since they bring the region about half of their annual warm season rainfall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Motion">Motion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thunderstorm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Motion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sturmfront_auf_Doppler-Radar-Schirm.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="864" data-file-width="1196" decoding="async" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Sturmfront_auf_Doppler-Radar-Schirm.jpg/220px-Sturmfront_auf_Doppler-Radar-Schirm.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Sturmfront_auf_Doppler-Radar-Schirm.jpg/330px-Sturmfront_auf_Doppler-Radar-Schirm.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Sturmfront_auf_Doppler-Radar-Schirm.jpg/440px-Sturmfront_auf_Doppler-Radar-Schirm.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sturmfront_auf_Doppler-Radar-Schirm.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Thunderstorm line viewed in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reflectivity" title="Reflectivity">reflectivity</a> (<a href="/wiki/DBZ_(meteorology)" title="DBZ (meteorology)">dBZ</a>) on a <a href="/wiki/Plan_position_indicator" title="Plan position indicator">plan position indicator</a> radar display</div></div></div>\n<p>The two major ways thunderstorms move are via <a href="/wiki/Advection" title="Advection">advection</a> of the wind and propagation along <a href="/wiki/Outflow_boundary" title="Outflow boundary">outflow boundaries</a> towards sources of greater heat and moisture. Many thunderstorms move with the mean wind speed through the Earth\'s <a href="/wiki/Troposphere" title="Troposphere">troposphere</a>, the lowest 8 kilometres (5.0&#160;mi) of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere" title="Earth\'s atmosphere">Earth\'s atmosphere</a>. Weaker thunderstorms are steered by winds closer to the Earth\'s surface than stronger thunderstorms, as the weaker thunderstorms are not as tall. Organized, long-lived thunderstorm cells and complexes move at a right angle to the direction of the vertical <a href="/wiki/Wind_shear" title="Wind shear">wind shear</a> vector. If the gust front, or leading edge of the outflow boundary, races ahead of the thunderstorm, its motion will accelerate in tandem. This is more of a factor with thunderstorms with heavy precipitation (HP) than with thunderstorms with low precipitation (LP). When thunderstorms merge, which is most likely when numerous thunderstorms exist in proximity to each other, the motion of the stronger thunderstorm normally dictates the future motion of the merged cell. The stronger the mean wind, the less likely other processes will be involved in storm motion. On <a href="/wiki/Weather_radar" title="Weather radar">weather radar</a>, storms are tracked by using a prominent feature and tracking it from scan to scan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-motion_13-3"><a href="#cite_note-motion-13">[13]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Back-building_thunderstorm">Back-building thunderstorm</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thunderstorm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Back-building thunderstorm">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>A back-building thunderstorm, commonly referred to as a <a href="/wiki/Training_(meteorology)" title="Training (meteorology)">training thunderstorm</a>, is a thunderstorm in which new development takes place on the upwind side (usually the west or southwest side in the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Hemisphere" title="Northern Hemisphere">Northern Hemisphere</a>), such that the storm seems to remain stationary or propagate in a backward direction. Though the storm often appears stationary on radar, or even moving upwind, this is an illusion. The storm is really a multi-cell storm with new, more vigorous cells that form on the upwind side, replacing older cells that continue to drift downwind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> When this happens, catastrophic flooding is possible. In <a href="/wiki/Rapid_City,_South_Dakota" title="Rapid City, South Dakota">Rapid City, South Dakota</a>, in 1972, an unusual alignment of winds at various levels of the atmosphere combined to produce a continuously training set of cells that dropped an enormous quantity of rain upon the same area, resulting in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Black_Hills_flood" title="Black Hills flood">devastating flash flooding</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> A similar event occurred in <a href="/wiki/Boscastle" title="Boscastle">Boscastle</a>, England, on 16 August 2004,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> and over Chennai on 1 December 2015 <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Hazards">Hazards</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thunderstorm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Hazards">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Each year, many people are killed or seriously injured by severe thunderstorms despite the advance warning. While severe thunderstorms are most common in the <a href="/wiki/Spring_(season)" title="Spring (season)">spring</a> and <a href="/wiki/Summer" title="Summer">summer</a>, they can occur at just about any time of the year.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cloud-to-ground_lightning">Cloud-to-ground lightning</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thunderstorm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Cloud-to-ground lightning">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Lightning_strike" title="Lightning strike">Lightning strike</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wildfire" title="Wildfire">Wildfire</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Blitze_IMGP6376_wp.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2528" data-file-width="3204" decoding="async" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Blitze_IMGP6376_wp.jpg/220px-Blitze_IMGP6376_wp.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Blitze_IMGP6376_wp.jpg/330px-Blitze_IMGP6376_wp.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Blitze_IMGP6376_wp.jpg/440px-Blitze_IMGP6376_wp.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Blitze_IMGP6376_wp.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A return stroke, cloud-to-ground lightning strike during a thunderstorm.</div></div></div>\n<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cloud-to-ground_lightning" title="Cloud-to-ground lightning">Cloud-to-ground lightning</a> frequently occurs within the phenomena of thunderstorms and have numerous hazards towards landscapes and populations. One of the more significant hazards lightning can pose is the wildfires they are capable of igniting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scott2000_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Scott2000-48">[48]</a></sup> Under a regime of low precipitation (LP) thunderstorms, where little precipitation is present, rainfall cannot prevent fires from starting when vegetation is dry as lightning produces a concentrated amount of extreme heat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ufl_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-ufl-49">[49]</a></sup>  Direct damage caused by lightning strikes occurs on occasion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> In areas with a high frequency for cloud-to-ground lightning, like <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a>, lightning causes several fatalities per year, most commonly to people working outside.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Acid rain is also a frequent risk produced by lightning. <a href="/wiki/Distilled_water" title="Distilled water">Distilled water</a> has a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neutral_solution" title="Neutral solution">neutral</a> <a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">pH</a> of 7.  &#8220;Clean&#8221; or unpolluted rain has a slightly acidic pH of about 5.2, because carbon dioxide and water in the air react together to form <a href="/wiki/Carbonic_acid" title="Carbonic acid">carbonic acid</a>, a weak acid (pH 5.6 in distilled water), but unpolluted rain also contains other chemicals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nitric_oxide" title="Nitric oxide">Nitric oxide</a> present during thunderstorm phenomena,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> caused by the oxidation of atmospheric nitrogen, can result in the production of acid rain, if nitric oxide forms compounds with the water molecules in precipitation, thus creating acid rain. Acid rain can damage infrastructures containing calcite or certain other solid chemical compounds. In ecosystems, acid rain can dissolve plant tissues of vegetations and increase acidification process in bodies of water and in <a href="/wiki/Soil" title="Soil">soil</a>, resulting in deaths of marine and terrestrial organisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EPA_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-EPA-54">[54]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hail">Hail</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thunderstorm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Hail">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hail" title="Hail">Hail</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bogota_hailstorm.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Bogota_hailstorm.jpg/220px-Bogota_hailstorm.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Bogota_hailstorm.jpg/330px-Bogota_hailstorm.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Bogota_hailstorm.jpg/440px-Bogota_hailstorm.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bogota_hailstorm.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Hailstorm in <a href="/wiki/Bogot%C3%A1" title="Bogot&#225;">Bogot&#225;</a>, <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">Colombia</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Any thunderstorm that produces hail that reaches the ground is known as a hailstorm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> Thunderclouds that are capable of producing hailstones are often seen obtaining green coloration. Hail is more common along mountain ranges because mountains force horizontal winds upwards (known as <a href="/wiki/Orographic_lift" title="Orographic lift">orographic lifting</a>), thereby intensifying the updrafts within thunderstorms and making hail more likely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>  One of the more common regions for large hail is across mountainous northern India, which reported one of the highest hail-related death tolls on record in 1888.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oliver_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Oliver-57">[57]</a></sup> China also experiences significant hailstorms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>  Across <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Croatia" title="Croatia">Croatia</a> experiences frequent occurrences of hail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a>, hail is most common in the area where <a href="/wiki/Colorado" title="Colorado">Colorado</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nebraska" title="Nebraska">Nebraska</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Wyoming" title="Wyoming">Wyoming</a> meet, known as "Hail Alley".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ncarhail_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-ncarhail-60">[60]</a></sup>  Hail in this region occurs between the months of March and October during the afternoon and evening hours, with the bulk of the occurrences from May through September. <a href="/wiki/Cheyenne,_Wyoming" title="Cheyenne, Wyoming">Cheyenne, Wyoming</a> is North America\'s most hail-prone city with an average of nine to ten hailstorms per season.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nolanhail_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nolanhail-61">[61]</a></sup> In South America, areas prone to hail are cities like Bogot&#225;, Colombia.\n</p><p>Hail can cause serious damage, notably to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Automobile" title="Automobile">automobiles</a>, aircraft, skylights, glass-roofed structures, <a href="/wiki/Livestock" title="Livestock">livestock</a>, and most commonly, farmers\' <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crops" title="Crops">crops</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nolanhail_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nolanhail-61">[61]</a></sup>  Hail is one of the most significant thunderstorm hazards to aircraft. When hail stones exceed 13 millimetres (0.5&#160;in) in diameter, planes can be seriously damaged within seconds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> The hailstones accumulating on the ground can also be hazardous to landing aircraft. Wheat, corn, soybeans, and tobacco are the most sensitive crops to hail damage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oliver_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-Oliver-57">[57]</a></sup>  Hail is one of Canada\'s most costly hazards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> Hailstorms have been the cause of costly and deadly events throughout history. One of the earliest recorded incidents occurred around the 9th century in <a href="/wiki/Roopkund" title="Roopkund">Roopkund</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uttarakhand" title="Uttarakhand">Uttarakhand</a>, India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> The largest hailstone in terms of maximum circumference and length ever recorded in the United States fell in 2003 in <a href="/wiki/Aurora,_Nebraska" title="Aurora, Nebraska">Aurora, Nebraska</a>, United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tornadoes_and_waterspouts">Tornadoes and waterspouts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thunderstorm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Tornadoes and waterspouts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:F5_tornado_Elie_Manitoba_2007.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="2592" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/F5_tornado_Elie_Manitoba_2007.jpg/220px-F5_tornado_Elie_Manitoba_2007.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/F5_tornado_Elie_Manitoba_2007.jpg/330px-F5_tornado_Elie_Manitoba_2007.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/F5_tornado_Elie_Manitoba_2007.jpg/440px-F5_tornado_Elie_Manitoba_2007.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:F5_tornado_Elie_Manitoba_2007.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In June 2007, the town of <a href="/wiki/Elie,_Manitoba" title="Elie, Manitoba">Elie, Manitoba</a> was struck by an <a href="/wiki/Fujita_scale" title="Fujita scale">F5</a> <a href="/wiki/2007_Elie,_Manitoba_tornado" title="2007 Elie, Manitoba tornado">tornado</a>.</div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Tornado" title="Tornado">Tornado</a> and <a href="/wiki/Waterspout" title="Waterspout">Waterspout</a></div>\n<p>A tornado is a violent, rotating column of air in contact with both the surface of the earth and a cumulonimbus cloud (otherwise known as a thundercloud) or, in rare cases, the base of a <a href="/wiki/Cumulus_cloud" title="Cumulus cloud">cumulus cloud</a>. Tornadoes come in many sizes but are typically in the form of a visible <a href="/wiki/Funnel_cloud" title="Funnel cloud">condensation funnel</a>, whose narrow end touches the earth and is often encircled by a cloud of <a href="/wiki/Debris" title="Debris">debris</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dust" title="Dust">dust</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Renno_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Renno-66">[66]</a></sup>  Most tornadoes have wind speeds between 40 and 110&#160;mph (64 and 177&#160;km/h), are approximately 75 metres (246&#160;ft) across, and travel several kilometers (a few miles) before dissipating. Some attain wind speeds of more than 300&#160;mph (480&#160;km/h), stretch more than 1,600 metres (1&#160;mi) across, and stay on the ground for more than 100&#160;kilometres (dozens of miles).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SPC_FAQ_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-SPC_FAQ-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fastest_wind_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-fastest_wind-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-widest_tornado_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-widest_tornado-69">[69]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Fujita_scale" title="Fujita scale">Fujita scale</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enhanced_Fujita_Scale" title="Enhanced Fujita Scale">Enhanced Fujita Scale</a> rate tornadoes by damage caused. An <b>EF0</b> tornado, the weakest category, damages trees but not substantial structures. An <b>EF5</b> tornado, the strongest category, rips buildings off their foundations and can deform large <a href="/wiki/Skyscraper" title="Skyscraper">skyscrapers</a>. The similar <a href="/wiki/TORRO_scale" title="TORRO scale">TORRO scale</a> ranges from a <b>T0</b> for extremely weak tornadoes to <b>T11</b> for the most powerful known tornadoes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pulse-Doppler_radar" title="Pulse-Doppler radar">Doppler</a> <a href="/wiki/Weather_radar" title="Weather radar">radar</a> data, <a href="/wiki/Photogrammetry" title="Photogrammetry">photogrammetry</a>, and ground swirl patterns (cycloidal marks) may also be analyzed to determine intensity and award a rating.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EF_SPC_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-EF_SPC-71">[71]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Great_Lakes_Waterspouts.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="372" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="136" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/41/Great_Lakes_Waterspouts.jpg/220px-Great_Lakes_Waterspouts.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/41/Great_Lakes_Waterspouts.jpg/330px-Great_Lakes_Waterspouts.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/41/Great_Lakes_Waterspouts.jpg/440px-Great_Lakes_Waterspouts.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Great_Lakes_Waterspouts.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Formation of numerous waterspouts in the <a href="/wiki/Great_Lakes" title="Great Lakes">Great Lakes</a> region. (North America)</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kings_Christian_Church_carpark_Flooded.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="895" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="123" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Kings_Christian_Church_carpark_Flooded.jpg/220px-Kings_Christian_Church_carpark_Flooded.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Kings_Christian_Church_carpark_Flooded.jpg/330px-Kings_Christian_Church_carpark_Flooded.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Kings_Christian_Church_carpark_Flooded.jpg/440px-Kings_Christian_Church_carpark_Flooded.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kings_Christian_Church_carpark_Flooded.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A flash flood caused by a severe thunderstorm</div></div></div>\n<p>Waterspouts have similar characteristics as tornadoes, characterized by a spiraling funnel-shaped wind current that form over bodies of water, connecting to large cumulonimbus clouds. Waterspouts are generally classified as forms of tornadoes, or more specifically, non-<a href="/wiki/Supercell" title="Supercell">supercelled</a> tornadoes that develop over large bodies of water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> These spiralling columns of air frequently develop within tropical areas close to the <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">equator</a>, but are less common within areas of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High_latitude" title="High latitude">high latitude</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flash_flood">Flash flood</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thunderstorm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Flash flood">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Flash_flood" title="Flash flood">Flash flood</a></div>\n<p>Flash flooding is the process where a landscape, most notably an urban environment, is subjected to rapid floods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup>  These rapid floods occur more quickly and are more localized than seasonal river flooding or areal flooding<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> and are frequently (though not always) associated with intense rainfall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> Flash flooding can frequently occur in slow-moving thunderstorms and is usually caused by the heavy liquid precipitation that accompanies it. Flash floods are most common in densely populated urban environments, where few plants and bodies of water are present to absorb and contain the extra water. Flash flooding can be hazardous to small infrastructure, such as bridges, and weakly constructed buildings. Plants and crops in agricultural areas can be destroyed and devastated by the force of raging water. Automobiles parked within affected areas can also be displaced. <a href="/wiki/Soil" title="Soil">Soil</a> erosion can occur as well, exposing risks of <a href="/wiki/Landslide" title="Landslide">landslide</a> phenomena.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Downburst">Downburst</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thunderstorm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Downburst">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Downburst" title="Downburst">Downburst</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Downburst_damage.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Downburst_damage.jpg/220px-Downburst_damage.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Downburst_damage.jpg/330px-Downburst_damage.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Downburst_damage.jpg/440px-Downburst_damage.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Downburst_damage.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Trees uprooted or displaced by the force of a downburst wind in northwest Monroe County, <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin" title="Wisconsin">Wisconsin</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Downburst winds can produce numerous hazards to landscapes experiencing thunderstorms. Downburst winds are generally very powerful, and are often mistaken for wind speeds produced by tornadoes,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-erh.noaa_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-erh.noaa-77">[77]</a></sup> due to the concentrated amount of force exerted by their straight-horizontal characteristic. Downburst winds can be hazardous to unstable, incomplete, or weakly constructed infrastructures and buildings. Agricultural crops, and other plants in nearby environments can be uprooted and damaged. Aircraft engaged in takeoff or landing can crash.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Extreme_Weather_9-3"><a href="#cite_note-Extreme_Weather-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-erh.noaa_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-erh.noaa-77">[77]</a></sup> Automobiles can be displaced by the force exerted by downburst winds. Downburst winds are usually formed in areas when high pressure air systems of downdrafts begin to sink and displace the air masses below it, due to their higher density. When these downdrafts reach the surface, they spread out and turn into the destructive straight-horizontal winds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Extreme_Weather_9-4"><a href="#cite_note-Extreme_Weather-9">[9]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Thunderstorm_asthma">Thunderstorm asthma</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thunderstorm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Thunderstorm asthma">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Thunderstorm_asthma" title="Thunderstorm asthma">Thunderstorm asthma</a></div>\n<p>Thunderstorm asthma is the triggering of an asthma attack by environmental conditions directly caused by a local thunderstorm. During a thunderstorm, pollen grains can absorb moisture and then burst into much smaller fragments with these fragments being easily dispersed by wind. While larger pollen grains are usually filtered by hairs in the nose, the smaller pollen fragments are able to pass through and enter the lungs, triggering the asthma attack.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Safety_precautions">Safety precautions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thunderstorm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Safety precautions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Emergency_management" title="Emergency management">Emergency management</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tornado_preparedness" title="Tornado preparedness">Tornado preparedness</a></div>\n<p>Most thunderstorms come and go fairly uneventfully; however, any thunderstorm can become <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Severe_thunderstorm" title="Severe thunderstorm">severe</a>, and all thunderstorms, by definition, present the danger of <a href="/wiki/Lightning" title="Lightning">lightning</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-redcross_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-redcross-82">[82]</a></sup> Thunderstorm preparedness and safety refers to taking steps before, during, and after a thunderstorm to minimize injury and damage.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Preparedness">Preparedness</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thunderstorm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Preparedness">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Preparedness refers to precautions that should be taken before a thunderstorm. Some preparedness takes the form of general readiness (as a thunderstorm can occur at any time of the day or year).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NWS_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-NWS-83">[83]</a></sup> Preparing a family emergency plan, for example, can save valuable time if a storm arises quickly and unexpectedly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> Preparing the home by removing dead or rotting limbs and trees, which can be blown over in high winds, can also significantly reduce the risk of property damage and personal injury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fema_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-fema-85">[85]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/National_Weather_Service" title="National Weather Service">National Weather Service</a> (NWS) in the United States recommends several precautions that people should take if thunderstorms are likely to occur:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NWS_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-NWS-83">[83]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dd><ul><li>Know the names of local counties, cities, and towns, as these are how warnings are described.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NWS_83-2"><a href="#cite_note-NWS-83">[83]</a></sup></li>\n<li>Monitor forecasts and weather conditions and know whether thunderstorms are likely in the area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-86">[86]</a></sup></li>\n<li>Be alert for natural signs of an approaching storm.</li>\n<li>Cancel or reschedule outdoor events (to avoid being caught outdoors when a storm hits).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-86">[86]</a></sup></li>\n<li>Take action early so you have time to get to a safe place.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_86-2"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-86">[86]</a></sup></li>\n<li>Get inside a substantial building or hard-topped metal vehicle before threatening weather arrives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_86-3"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-86">[86]</a></sup></li>\n<li>If you hear <a href="/wiki/Thunder" title="Thunder">thunder</a>, get to the safe place immediately.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_86-4"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-86">[86]</a></sup></li>\n<li>Avoid open areas like hilltops, fields, and <a href="/wiki/Beach" title="Beach">beaches</a>, and don\'t be or be near the tallest objects in an area when thunderstorms are occurring.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NWS_83-3"><a href="#cite_note-NWS-83">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_86-5"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-86">[86]</a></sup></li>\n<li>Don\'t shelter under tall or isolated trees during thunderstorms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_86-6"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-86">[86]</a></sup></li>\n<li>If in the woods, put as much distance as possible between you and any trees during thunderstorms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_86-7"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-86">[86]</a></sup></li>\n<li>If in a group, spread out to increase the chances of survivors who could come to the aid of any victims from a <a href="/wiki/Lightning_strike" title="Lightning strike">lightning strike</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_86-8"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-86">[86]</a></sup></li></ul></dd></dl>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Safety">Safety</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thunderstorm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Safety">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>While safety and preparedness often overlap, &#8220;thunderstorm safety&#8221; generally refers to what people should do during and after a storm. The <a href="/wiki/American_Red_Cross" title="American Red Cross">American Red Cross</a> recommends that people follow these precautions if a storm is imminent or in progress:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-redcross_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-redcross-82">[82]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dd><ul><li>Take action immediately upon hearing thunder.
