Anyone close enough to the storm to hear thunder can be struck by lightning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fema_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-fema-85">[85]</a></sup></li>\n<li>Avoid electrical appliances, including corded telephones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-redcross_82-2"><a href="#cite_note-redcross-82">[82]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cordless_telephone" title="Cordless telephone">Cordless</a> and wireless telephones are safe to use during a thunderstorm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fema_85-2"><a href="#cite_note-fema-85">[85]</a></sup></li>\n<li>Close and stay away from windows and doors, as glass can become a serious hazard in high wind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-redcross_82-3"><a href="#cite_note-redcross-82">[82]</a></sup></li>\n<li>Do not bathe or shower, as plumbing conducts <a href="/wiki/Electricity" title="Electricity">electricity</a>.</li>\n<li>If driving, safely exit the roadway, turn on hazard lights, and park. Remain in the vehicle and avoid touching metal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-redcross_82-4"><a href="#cite_note-redcross-82">[82]</a></sup></li></ul></dd></dl>\n<p>The NWS stopped recommending the "lightning crouch" in 2008 as it doesn\'t provide a significant level of protection and will not significantly lower the risk of being killed or injured from a nearby lightning strike.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_86-9"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Frequent_occurrences">Frequent occurrences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thunderstorm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Frequent occurrences">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/United_States_rainfall_climatology" title="United States rainfall climatology">United States rainfall climatology</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cumulonimbus_Over_Niagara_Falls.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Cumulonimbus_Over_Niagara_Falls.JPG/220px-Cumulonimbus_Over_Niagara_Falls.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Cumulonimbus_Over_Niagara_Falls.JPG/330px-Cumulonimbus_Over_Niagara_Falls.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Cumulonimbus_Over_Niagara_Falls.JPG/440px-Cumulonimbus_Over_Niagara_Falls.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cumulonimbus_Over_Niagara_Falls.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A mild thunderstorm over <a href="/wiki/Niagara_Falls,_Ontario" title="Niagara Falls, Ontario">Niagara Falls, Ontario</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Thunderstorms occur throughout the world, even in the polar regions, with the greatest frequency in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tropical" title="Tropical">tropical</a> <a href="/wiki/Rainforest" title="Rainforest">rainforest</a> areas, where they may occur nearly daily. At any given time approximately 2,000 thunderstorms are occurring on Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Kampala" title="Kampala">Kampala</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tororo" title="Tororo">Tororo</a> in <a href="/wiki/Uganda" title="Uganda">Uganda</a> have each been mentioned as the most thunderous places on Earth,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> a claim also made for <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bogor" title="Bogor">Bogor</a> on the Indonesian island of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Java_(island)" title="Java (island)">Java</a>. Other cities known for frequent storm activity include <a href="/wiki/Darwin,_Northern_Territory" title="Darwin, Northern Territory">Darwin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Caracas" title="Caracas">Caracas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manila" title="Manila">Manila</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mumbai" title="Mumbai">Mumbai</a>. Thunderstorms are associated with the various <a href="/wiki/Monsoon" title="Monsoon">monsoon</a> seasons around the globe, and they populate the <a href="/wiki/Rainband" title="Rainband">rainbands</a> of <a href="/wiki/Tropical_cyclone" title="Tropical cyclone">tropical cyclones</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> In temperate regions, they are most frequent in spring and summer, although they can occur along or ahead of <a href="/wiki/Cold_front" title="Cold front">cold fronts</a> at any time of year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DR_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-DR-92">[92]</a></sup>  They may also occur within a cooler air mass following the passage of a cold front over a relatively warmer body of water. Thunderstorms are rare in polar regions because of cold surface temperatures.\n</p><p>Some of the most powerful thunderstorms over the United States occur in the Midwest and the <a href="/wiki/Southern_United_States" title="Southern United States">Southern states</a>. These storms can produce large hail and powerful tornadoes. Thunderstorms are relatively uncommon along much of the <a href="/wiki/West_Coast_of_the_United_States" title="West Coast of the United States">West Coast of the United States</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> but they occur with greater frequency in the inland areas, particularly the <a href="/wiki/Sacramento_Valley" title="Sacramento Valley">Sacramento</a> and <a href="/wiki/San_Joaquin_Valley" title="San Joaquin Valley">San Joaquin</a> Valleys of <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a>. In spring and summer, they occur nearly daily in certain areas of the <a href="/wiki/Rocky_Mountains" title="Rocky Mountains">Rocky Mountains</a> as part of the <a href="/wiki/North_American_Monsoon" title="North American Monsoon">North American Monsoon</a> regime. In the <a href="/wiki/Northeastern_United_States" title="Northeastern United States">Northeast</a>, storms take on similar characteristics and patterns as the Midwest, but with less frequency and severity. During the summer, <a href="/wiki/Air-mass_thunderstorm" title="Air-mass thunderstorm">air-mass thunderstorms</a> are an almost daily occurrence over central and southern parts of <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy">Energy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thunderstorm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Energy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Sprite_(lightning)" title="Sprite (lightning)">Sprite (lightning)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Upper-atmospheric_lightning" title="Upper-atmospheric lightning">Upper-atmospheric lightning</a>, and <a href="/wiki/St._Elmo%27s_fire" title="St. Elmo\'s fire">St. Elmo\'s fire</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:How_thunderstorms_launch_particle_beams_into_space_300dpi.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3613" data-file-width="5500" decoding="async" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/How_thunderstorms_launch_particle_beams_into_space_300dpi.jpg/220px-How_thunderstorms_launch_particle_beams_into_space_300dpi.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/How_thunderstorms_launch_particle_beams_into_space_300dpi.jpg/330px-How_thunderstorms_launch_particle_beams_into_space_300dpi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/How_thunderstorms_launch_particle_beams_into_space_300dpi.jpg/440px-How_thunderstorms_launch_particle_beams_into_space_300dpi.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:How_thunderstorms_launch_particle_beams_into_space_300dpi.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>How thunderstorms launch particle beams into space</div></div></div>\n<p>If the quantity of water that is condensed in and subsequently precipitated from a cloud is known, then the total energy of a thunderstorm can be calculated. In a typical thunderstorm, approximately 5&#215;10<sup>8</sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kilograms" title="Kilograms">kg</a> of water vapor are lifted, and the amount of <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a> released when this condenses is 10<sup>15</sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joules" title="Joules">joules</a>. This is on the same order of magnitude of energy released within a tropical cyclone, and more energy than that released during <a href="/wiki/Atomic_bombings_of_Hiroshima_and_Nagasaki" title="Atomic bombings of Hiroshima and Nagasaki">the atomic bomb blast at Hiroshima, Japan in 1945</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-condensationenergy_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-condensationenergy-11">[11]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fermi_Gamma-ray_Burst_Monitor" title="Fermi Gamma-ray Burst Monitor">Fermi Gamma-ray Burst Monitor</a> results show that <a href="/wiki/Gamma_ray" title="Gamma ray">gamma rays</a> and <a href="/wiki/Antimatter" title="Antimatter">antimatter</a> particles (<a href="/wiki/Positron" title="Positron">positrons</a>) can be generated in powerful thunderstorms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> It is suggested that the antimatter positrons are formed in <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_gamma-ray_flash" title="Terrestrial gamma-ray flash">terrestrial gamma-ray flashes</a> (TGF). TGFs are brief bursts occurring inside thunderstorms and associated with lightning. The streams of positrons and electrons collide higher in the atmosphere to generate more gamma rays.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> About 500 TGFs may occur every day worldwide, but mostly go undetected.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Studies">Studies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thunderstorm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Studies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>In more contemporary times, thunderstorms have taken on the role of a scientific curiosity. Every spring, <a href="/wiki/Storm_chasing" title="Storm chasing">storm chasers</a> head to the <a href="/wiki/Great_Plains" title="Great Plains">Great Plains</a> of the United States and the Canadian Prairies to explore the scientific aspects of storms and tornadoes through use of videotaping.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> Radio pulses produced by cosmic rays are being used to study how electric charges develop within thunderstorms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> More organized meteorological projects such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/VORTEX2" title="VORTEX2">VORTEX2</a> use an array of sensors, such as the <a href="/wiki/Doppler_on_Wheels" title="Doppler on Wheels">Doppler on Wheels</a>, vehicles with mounted automated <a href="/wiki/Weather_station" title="Weather station">weather stations</a>, <a href="/wiki/Weather_balloon" title="Weather balloon">weather balloons</a>, and unmanned aircraft to investigate thunderstorms expected to produce severe weather.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup>  Lightning is detected remotely using sensors that detect cloud-to-ground lightning strokes with 95&#160;percent accuracy in detection and within 250 metres (820&#160;ft) of their point of origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mythology_and_religion">Mythology and religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thunderstorm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Mythology and religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Thunderstorms strongly influenced many early civilizations. <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Greeks</a> believed that they were battles waged by <a href="/wiki/Zeus" title="Zeus">Zeus</a>, who hurled lightning bolts forged by <a href="/wiki/Hephaestus" title="Hephaestus">Hephaestus</a>. Some <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous peoples of the Americas">American Indian</a> tribes associated thunderstorms with the <a href="/wiki/Thunderbird_(mythology)" title="Thunderbird (mythology)">Thunderbird</a>, who they believed was a servant of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Spirit" title="Great Spirit">Great Spirit</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Norsemen" title="Norsemen">Norse</a> considered thunderstorms to occur when <a href="/wiki/Thor" title="Thor">Thor</a> went to fight <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B6tunn" title="J&#246;tunn">J&#246;tnar</a>, with the thunder and lightning being the effect of his strikes with the hammer <a href="/wiki/Mj%C3%B6lnir" title="Mj&#246;lnir">Mj&#246;lnir</a>. <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a> recognizes <a href="/wiki/Indra" title="Indra">Indra</a> as the god of rain and thunderstorms. <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christian</a> doctrine accepts that fierce storms are the work of <a href="/wiki/God" title="God">God</a>. These ideas were still within the mainstream as late as the 18th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther" title="Martin Luther">Martin Luther</a> was out walking when a thunderstorm began, causing him to pray to God for being saved and promising to become a monk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Outside_of_Earth">Outside of Earth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thunderstorm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Outside of Earth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Thunderstorms, evidenced by flashes of <a href="/wiki/Lightning" title="Lightning">lightning</a>, on Jupiter have been detected and are associated with clouds where water may exist as both a liquid and ice, suggesting a mechanism similar to that on Earth. (Water is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polar_molecule" title="Polar molecule">polar molecule</a> that can carry a charge, so it is capable of creating the charge separation needed to produce lightning.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-elkins-tanton_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-elkins-tanton-102">[102]</a></sup> These electrical discharges can be up to a thousand times more powerful than lightning on the Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> The water clouds can form thunderstorms driven by the heat rising from the interior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup>  The clouds of Venus may also be capable of producing <a href="/wiki/Lightning" title="Lightning">lightning</a>; some observations suggest that the lightning rate is at least half of that on Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Russell2007_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-Russell2007-105">[105]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thunderstorm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Barber%27s_pole#Meteorology" title="Barber\'s pole">Barber\'s pole</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Continuous_gusts" title="Continuous gusts">Continuous gusts</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Convective_storm_detection" title="Convective storm detection">Convective storm detection</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Hector_(cloud)" title="Hector (cloud)">Hector (cloud)</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Severe_thunderstorm_warning" title="Severe thunderstorm warning">Severe thunderstorm warning</a> and <a href="/wiki/Severe_thunderstorm_watch" title="Severe thunderstorm watch">Severe thunderstorm watch</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Thundersnow" title="Thundersnow">Thundersnow</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Tornado_warning" title="Tornado warning">Tornado warning</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Tornado_watch" title="Tornado watch">Tornado watch</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Training_(meteorology)" title="Training (meteorology)">Training (meteorology)</a></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thunderstorm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">\n<ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a class="external text" href="http://www.weather.gov/glossary/index.php?letter=t" rel="nofollow">"Weather Glossary &#8211; T"</a>. National Weather Service. 21 April 2005<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">2006-08-23</span></span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r879151008">.mw-parser-output cite.citation{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .citation q{quotes:"\\"""\\"""\'""\'"}.mw-parser-output .citation .cs1-lock-free
